## Transcript

- [00:01] Smarty pants, egg head, brainiac.
- [00:04] You've heard terms like these before, maybe you've even been on the receiving end of one
- [00:08] of them.
- [00:09] But actually, defining intelligence is a lot trickier than just coming up with new names
- [00:13] for smart people.
- [00:14] I mean, intelligence isn't like height or weight; you can't just toss them on a scale
- [00:17] and give it an exact measurement.
- [00:18] It has different meanings for different cultures and ages and skill sets.
- [00:22] So what is intelligence?
- [00:23] It's a question that doesn't give us a lot of answers, but it does open a bunch of other
- [00:28] equally important and interesting questions.
- [00:31] Like, what influences it?
- [00:32] And how can it be assessed?
- [00:34] Is it a single, general ability, or does it cover a range of aptitudes and skills and
- [00:38] talents?
- [00:39] How do things like creativity and innovation factor in?
- [00:41] Or genetics or environment, or education?
- [00:43] And what about emotional intelligence?
- [00:45] Most agree that it's best to think of intelligence not of a concrete thing so much as a concept,
- [00:50] the ability to learn from experience, solve problems, and use knowledge to adapt to new
- [00:54] experiences.
- [00:55] We've often used intelligence tests, to assess and compare mental aptitude, but these tests
- [00:59] have a long, complex and dark history.
- [01:02] I mean there are Nazis involved so, yeah.
- [01:05] So as you'll see, there are reasons that intelligence is one of the most hotly debated subjects
- [01:09] in psychology.
- [01:10] It's complicated and controversial.
- [01:17] [Intro]
- [01:23] What if I'm the world's greatest Rubik's cube solver but a terrible speller?
- [01:26] Or a truly gifted artist who's barely mastered long division?
- [01:30] Could anyone say I was intelligent or not based on those different aptitudes, or would
- [01:34] it be more accurate to measure my brainpower on several different scales?
- [01:37] Around the turn of the twentieth century, British psychologist Charles Spearman suggested
- [01:41] that yes, we do have one comprehensive general intelligence that underlies all of specific
- [01:46] mental abilities.
- [01:47] He called it the G-Factor.
- [01:49] Spearman conceded that while people may have special talents like basket weaving or saxophone
- [01:53] solos or doing crossword puzzles, those things still fell under "G".
- [01:58] And he helped develop a statistical procedure called factor analysis to try to determine
- [02:02] how certain clusters of skills might correlate with another one.
- [02:05] Like, say someone who tests well in spatial skills might be good with numbers.
- [02:09] We might then refer to that cluster of skills, that factor, as spatial-numeric reasoning.
- [02:14] But to Spearman, the G-factor was something of an uber-factor connected to all intelligent
- [02:19] behavior from architecture to healing to survival skills, and it's why people who do well on
- [02:24] one kind of cognitive test tend to do well on others.
- [02:27] But as you can imagine, reducing intelligence to a single numerical test score was and is
- [02:33] problematic.
- [02:34] L.L.
- [02:35] Thurstone, an American pioneer of psychometrics and one of Spearman's first challengers, was
- [02:39] not into ranking people on a single scale.
- [02:41] Thurstone administered 56 different tests to his subjects then used them to identify
- [02:46] seven clusters of mental abilities.
- [02:48] By this system, you might turn out to be great at like verbal comprehension but less stellar
- [02:53] at something like numerical ability.
- [02:54] Sounds fair.
- [02:55] But when researchers followed up on his findings, they actually did see that high scores in
- [02:58] one aptitude usually meant good scores in the others, essentially backing up some evidence
- [03:03] for some kind of G-factor.
- [03:05] Even though their ideas did not often align, Spearman and Thurstone together paved the
- [03:09] way for more contemporary theories on intelligence.
- [03:12] For example, American psychologist Howard Gardner views intelligence as multiple abilities
- [03:16] that come in different forms.
- [03:17] He references instances of brain damage where one ability may be destroyed while others
- [03:21] stay perfectly intact.
- [03:23] Savants usually have some limited metal abilities but one exceptional ability when it comes
- [03:27] to like, computing figures or memorizing the complete works of Shakespeare.
- [03:30] To Gardner, this suggests that we have multiple intelligences beyond the G-factor.
- [03:34] In fact, he believes that we have eight intelligences, ranging from our skills with numbers and words
- [03:40] to our ability to understand physical space and the natural world.
- [03:43] American psychologist Robert Sternberg tends to agree with Gardner, though he boils them
- [03:47] down into three intelligences: analytical, or problem-solving intelligence, creative
- [03:52] intelligence, or the ability to adapt to new situations, and practical intelligence for
- [03:57] everyday tasks.
- [03:58] Both of these models seem reasonable, too, and Gardner and Sternberg's work has helped
- [04:02] teachers appreciate students' variety of talents.
- [04:05] But research has suggested that even these different ways to be smart are also linked
- [04:09] by some underlying general intelligence factor.
- [04:12] So what about other less tangible forms of intelligence, like creativity, our ability
- [04:16] to produce ideas that are both novel and valuable?
- [04:20] How can a test that demands one correct answer account for more creative solutions, so-called
- [04:24] "divergent thinking".
- [04:25] Well, traditional intelligence tests can't, and so far, while we do have some tests that
- [04:30] look at creative potential, we don't have a standardized system for quantifying creativity.
- [04:35] But Sternberg and his colleagues have identified five main components of creativity, which
- [04:40] are useful for framing our understanding of what creative intelligence is and how it works.
- [04:45] If you go through the list, you know who I think is really great at almost all of them?
- [04:49] Sherlock Holmes.
- [04:50] Hear me out.
- [04:51] First we've got expertise, or a well-developed base of knowledge.
- [04:54] This just means knowing a lot about a lot.
- [04:56] Whether it's arcane poisons, jellyfish behavior, or how to recognize a secret passage behind
- [05:01] a book shelf, expertise provides the mind with all sorts of data to work with and combine
- [05:06] in new ways.
- [05:07] Obviously Sherlock has incredible imaginative thinking skills, too, which provide him with
- [05:11] the ability to see things in new ways, recognize patterns and make connections.
- [05:16] He loves nothing more than rehashing these breadcrumb trails for the dopey constables
- [05:20] at the end of the case.
- [05:21] Sternberg also thought a venturesome personality contributes to creativity.
- [05:24] By hanging around opium dens and chasing thugs and generally courting danger, Sherlock routinely
- [05:30] seeks new experiences, tolerates risk, and perseveres in overcoming obstacles.
- [05:34] And everyone knows he's driven by intrinsic motivation.
- [05:37] I mean, he wants to help the widow discover the thief and everything, but really, Sherlock
- [05:41] is driven by his own interest and sense of challenge.
- [05:45] He gets pleasure from the work itself.
- [05:47] And finally, Sherlock benefits from a creative environment which sparks, supports, and refines
- [05:52] his ideas.
- [05:53] For so affectionately maintaining this environment on Sherlock's behalf, we largely have Dr.
- [05:58] Watson to thank.
- [05:59] Sherlock was obviously an academic and creative genius, but he was pretty weak in another
- [06:03] form of intelligence: the emotional kind.
- [06:07] Emotional intelligence, defined in 1997 by psychologist Peter Salovey and John Mayer
- [06:11] -- no, not, not that one-- is the ability to perceive, understand, manage, and use emotions.
- [06:17] I don't know about you, but I know plenty of smart people who have a hard time processing
- [06:21] social information.
- [06:23] The most brilliant mathematician may struggle to communicate with colleagues, neighbors,
- [06:27] or staff at the local deli.
- [06:29] Likewise, Sherlock often annoys, offends, or even baffles those around him.
- [06:33] Perceiving emotions means being able to recognize them in faces, and even in music, film, and
- [06:39] stories.
- [06:40] Understanding emotions relates to being able to predict them and how they might change.
- [06:43] And managing emotions comes down to knowing how to appropriately express yourself in various
- [06:47] situations.
- [06:48] And finally, emotional intelligence also means using emotions to enable adaptive or creative
- [06:53] thinking; like knowing how to manage conflict or comfort a grieving friend or work well
- [06:57] with others.
- [06:58] Much like creative intelligence, emotional intelligence can be measured to some degree
- [07:01] through testing, but there's no standardized way to, like, assign a numerical value.
- [07:06] So if we can't perfectly quantify things like creativity or emotional smarts, how did we
- [07:11] come up with a way to measure intelligence?
- [07:14] Well, as I mentioned earlier, it's a sordid story.
- [07:17] The first attempts to do it in the western world began with English scientist Francis
- [07:20] Galton in the 1800s.
- [07:22] Taking a page from his famous cousin Charles Darwin's theories on natural selection, Galton
- [07:27] wondered how that premise might extend to humans' natural ability when it came to intelligence.
- [07:32] He suggested that our smarts have a lot to do with heredity, so if we encouraged smart
- [07:36] people to breed with each other, we could essentially create a master race of geniuses.
- [07:41] If that sounds a little sketchy, it's because it was, like, really, really sketchy!!
- [07:48] This study of how to selectively and supposedly improve the human population, especially by
- [07:53] encouraging breeding in some people and discouraging it in others, is called "eugenics".
- [07:58] A term Galton himself coined, and I'll get back to, in a minute.
- [08:02] But around the turn of the twentieth century when eugenics was taking off, the French government
- [08:05] mandated that all children must attend school.
- [08:08] Many of these kids had never been in a classroom and teachers wanted to figure out how they
- [08:12] could identify kids who needed extra help.
- [08:14] Enter Alfred Binet and Theodore Simon, two French psychologists who were commissioned
- [08:19] to develop a test to measure a child's so-called mental age.
- [08:22] The concept of a kid's mental age is essentially the level of performance associated with a
- [08:26] certain chronological age.
- [08:27] So if six year old Bruno tests as well as the average six year old, he'd have a mental
- [08:32] age of six.
- [08:33] Binet believed that his tests could measure a child's current mental abilities, but that
- [08:37] intelligence wasn't a fixed, inborn thing.
- [08:39] He believed a person's capabilities could be raised with proper attention, self-discipline
- [08:43] and practice.
- [08:44] In other words, he was no eugenicist.
- [08:47] He was hoping that his tests would improve children's education by identifying those
- [08:51] who needed extra attention.
- [08:52] But Binet also feared that these tests would, in the wrong hands, be used to do just the
- [08:57] opposite: labeling children as "lost causes", limiting their opportunities.
- [09:02] And wow, was he on to something because that is pretty much exactly what happened.
- [09:06] German psychologist William Stern used revisions of Binet and Simon's work to create the famous
- [09:11] intelligence quotient, or IQ measurement.
- [09:13] At the time, your IQ was simply your mental age, divided by your chronological age, multiplied
- [09:19] by a hundred.
- [09:20] So for example Bruno is six, and so is his mental age, so his IQ ranks at a hundred,
- [09:25] but his little sister Betty is a four year-old with a mental age of five, so her IQ would
- [09:29] be 125.
- [09:30] That formula works pretty well for measuring kids, but it falls apart when it comes to
- [09:34] adults who don't hit measurable developmental steps like kids do.
- [09:37] I mean there's no real difference between a mental age of 34 and 35.
- [09:41] But Stanford professor Lewis Terman started promoting the widespread use of intelligence
- [09:45] tests in the early 1900s, and with his help the US government began the world's first
- [09:50] massive ministration of intelligence tests, when it assessed World War I army recruits
- [09:55] and immigrants fresh off the boat.
- [09:57] Unlike Binet, Terman did use these numerical findings as a kind of label, and he thought
- [10:01] his tests could, as he put it: "ultimately result in curtailing the reproduction of feeble-mindedness".
- [10:08] This kind of testing played right into eugenicists' sensibilities, and soon the eugenics movement
- [10:12] in the US had a pretty good fanclub, raising money from the Carnegie's and Rockafeller's
- [10:16] and with proponents working at Harvard and Columbia and Cornell.
- [10:19] In the first half of the 21st century, intelligence tests were used to enforce the sterilization
- [10:24] of about 60,000 people, around a third of whom were in California.
- [10:28] Most were poor white women, often unwed mothers or prostitutes.
- [10:32] Other eugenics efforts persisted later into the century, and there is evidence of poor
- [10:36] African American, Native American, or Latina women being forcibly or covertly sterilized
- [10:41] in large numbers as recently as the 1970s.
- [10:43] But do you know who really loved their eugenics?
- [10:46] The Nazis.
- [10:47] Hitler and his cronies took the idea of intelligence testing to even darker conclusions.
- [10:51] The Nazis were all about selecting against so-called "feeble-mindedness" and other undesirable
- [10:56] traits as they sought to strengthen what they saw as their Aryan nation.
- [11:00] They sterilized or simply executed hundreds of thousands of victims based of their answers
- [11:04] to IQ test questions that were really more abut adhering to social norms than measuring
- [11:08] actual intelligence.
- [11:10] Questions like: "Who was Bismarck?"
- [11:11] and "What does Christmas signify?"
- [11:13] So you can see how this terrifying history still makes some people leery of how such
- [11:17] tests are administered, interpreted, and weighted.
- [11:20] Today we understand that intelligence, as defined by all the people we've talked about
- [11:24] here, does appear to be a real and measurable phenomenon.
- [11:27] But no one can say that they've disentangled all of the would-be genetic, environmental,
- [11:32] educational, and socio-economic components of it.
- [11:35] In the end, it's best to think of intelligence as something about which we've still got a
- [11:38] lot to learn.
- [11:39] And next week, we'll talk about how we test intelligence today and the problems we still
- [11:44] face in doing it.
- [11:45] Today, your intelligent mind learned about the history of how we think about and define
- [11:49] different types of intelligence, what the G-factor is, and how Sherlock Holmes is incredibly
- [11:54] intelligent but emotionally unintelligent.
